Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 980;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 73;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 932;
    client_max_body_size 1727488;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.17.8;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 0;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 525;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 36;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1304;
    client_max_body_size 826368;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1086;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1881;
    client_max_body_size 681984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1402;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 19;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 713;
    client_max_body_size 1016832;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'https://*.google.com';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1811;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 111;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1842;
    client_max_body_size 1705984;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/7.2.1;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1880;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 61;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1189;
    client_max_body_size 976896;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 0;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

	 No Anti-CSRF tokens were found in a HTML submission form.
A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1975;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 118;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1900;
    client_max_body_size 817152;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 0;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1695;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 35;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1804;
    client_max_body_size 1519616;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 0;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1216;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 91;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 2048;
    client_max_body_size 695296;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 90;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 915;
    client_max_body_size 1558528;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;

        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/7.2.1;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 0;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1216;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 91;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 2048;
    client_max_body_size 695296;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }

            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }

daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1810;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 111;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1842;
    client_max_body_size 1705984;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1086;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 603;
    client_max_body_size 1100800;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy.

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1880;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 61;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1189;
    client_max_body_size 975872;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 0;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy.

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1086;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1880;
    client_max_body_size 681984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1684;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 50;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1792;
    client_max_body_size 1236992;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low
Alert →
	 http://www.exampletfm.com/test/upload.html 
	  
	 The Anti-MIME-Sniffing header X-Content-Type-Options was not set to 'nosniff'. This allows older versions of Internet Explorer and Chrome to perform MIME-sniffing on the response body, potentially causing the response body to be interpreted and displayed as a content type other than the declared content type. Current (early 2014) and legacy versions of Firefox will use the declared content type (if one is set), rather than performing MIME-sniffing.

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1086;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 604;
    client_max_body_size 1100800;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1086;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1881;
    client_max_body_size 681984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. * Manually resetting nginx to stopped state

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1216;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 91;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 2048;
    client_max_body_size 695296;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1881;
    client_max_body_size 681984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1550;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 91;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 2048;
    client_max_body_size 694272;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1684;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 50;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1792;
    client_max_body_size 1236992;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1087;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1881;
    client_max_body_size 681984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. * Manually resetting nginx to stopped state

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1550;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 40;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1792;
    client_max_body_size 1236992;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1086;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1792;
    client_max_body_size 1236992;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1087;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 725;
    client_max_body_size 985088;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1086;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1881;
    client_max_body_size 985088;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1550;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 40;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1792;
    client_max_body_size 1236992;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By Django2.2;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'https://*.google.com';";
        }
    }
}

Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1550;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 40;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1792;
    client_max_body_size 1236992;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server caddy;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1086;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1881;
    client_max_body_size 681984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1550;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 40;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1881;
    client_max_body_size 681984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1550;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 40;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1792;
    client_max_body_size 1236992;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1086;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 1;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1881;
    client_max_body_size 681984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. * Manually resetting nginx to stopped state

Configuration → 
user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 525;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 36;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1304;
    client_max_body_size 826368;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options SAMEORIGIN;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection 1;
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'none'; frame-ancestors 'none';";
        }
    }
}

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1086;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1881;
    client_max_body_size 681984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options "";
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }

daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1086;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 106;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 603;
    client_max_body_size 1100800;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options DENY;
            add_header X-Powered-By PHP/5.3.3;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";

}

    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 50;
    disable_symlinks on;
    autoindex off;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1792;
    client_max_body_size 1238016;
    server_tokens off;
    gzip off;

            deny all;

            add_header Server caddy;

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. * Manually resetting nginx to stopped state

    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;

            deny all;

            deny all;

            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;

            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;

            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;

A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. * Manually resetting nginx to stopped state

user nginx;
pid /var/run/nginx.pid;
worker_processes 4;
daemon on;
error_log /tmp/nginx-error.log warn;
events {
    worker_connections 1086;
}
http {
    include /etc/nginx/mime.types;
    default_type application/octet-stream;
    access_log /tmp/nginx-access.log;
    sendfile on;
    keepalive_timeout 107;
    disable_symlinks off;
    autoindex on;
    send_timeout 0;
    large_client_header_buffers 4 1881;
    client_max_body_size 681984;
    server_tokens on;
    gzip on;
    log_format my_tracking $request_body;
    resolver 8.8.8.8 valid=30s;
    server {
        server_name www.exampletfm.com;
        listen 80;
        error_page 500 502 503 504 /50x.html;
        location ^~ /assets/public/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location ^~ /assets/assets/ {
            deny all;
        }
        location /form {
            access_log /tmp/access.log my_tracking;
        }
        location / {
            root /tester/site/;
            index index.html index.htm;
            add_header X-Frame-Options "ALLOW-FROM http://www.exampletfm.com/";
            add_header X-Powered-By nginx/1.16.0;
            add_header X-Content-Type-Options nosniff;
            add_header Server apache;
            add_header X-XSS-Protection "1; mode=block";
            add_header Content-Security-Policy "default-src 'self'; frame-ancestors 'self';";
        }
    }
}

	 <form action="../form" method="post" enctype="multipart/form-data"> 
	 No Anti-CSRF tokens were found in a HTML submission form.
A cross-site request forgery is an attack that involves forcing a victim to send an HTTP request to a target destination without their knowledge or intent in order to perform an action as the victim. The underlying cause is application functionality using predictable URL/form actions in a repeatable way. The nature of the attack is that CSRF exploits the trust that a web site has for a user. By contrast, cross-site scripting (XSS) exploits the trust that a user has for a web site. Like XSS, CSRF attacks are not necessarily cross-site, but they can be. Cross-site request forgery is also known as CSRF, XSRF, one-click attack, session riding, confused deputy, and sea surf.

CSRF attacks are effective in a number of situations, including:
    * The victim has an active session on the target site.
    * The victim is authenticated via HTTP auth on the target site.
    * The victim is on the same local network as the target site.

CSRF has primarily been used to perform an action against a target site using the victim's privileges, but recent techniques have been discovered to disclose information by gaining access to the response. The risk of information disclosure is dramatically increased when the target site is vulnerable to XSS, because XSS can be used as a platform for CSRF, allowing the attack to operate within the bounds of the same-origin policy. 
	 Low
